id="MJXp-Span-119">L</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-2"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mstyle lineleading=".25ex" rowspacing="2ex"><mstyle indentshiftfirst="0em" indentshift="1em" indentshiftlast="1em"><mtable displaystyle="true"><mtr><mtd><mi>β</mi><mrow><mo>=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mi>β</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi></msub><mo>+</mo><msub><mi>β</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">L</mi></msub></mstyle></mrow><mrow><mo>=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mn>4</mn><mi>ε</mi><mi>tan</mi><mspace width="0.25em"></mspace><mi>θ</mi><mo>+</mo><mfrac><mrow><mn>0.9</mn><msub><mi>λ</mi><mrow><mi>Cu</mi><mo>−</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">K</mi><msub><mi>α</mi><mn>1</mn></msub></mrow></msub></mrow><mrow><mi>L</mi><mi>cos</mi><mspace width="0.25em"></mspace><mi>θ</mi></mrow></mfrac></mstyle></mrow></mtd></mtr><mtr><mtd><mi>β</mi><mi>cos</mi><mspace width="0.25em"></mspace><mi>θ</mi><mrow><mo>=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mn>4</mn><mi>ε</mi><mi>sin</mi><mspace width="0.25em"></mspace><mi>θ</mi><mo>+</mo><mfrac><mrow><mn>0.9</mn><msub><mi>λ</mi><mrow><mi>Cu</mi><mo>−</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">K</mi><msub><mi>α</mi><mn>1</mn></msub></mrow></msub></mrow><mi>L</mi></mfrac></mstyle></mrow></mtd></mtr></mtable></mstyle></mstyle></mstyle></mrow></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p">By constructing a Williamson–Hall plot, which illustrates the relationship between β cos θ and 4sin θ, the average crystallite size (<i>L</i>) and microstrain (ε) of a material can be estimated from the intercept value with the vertical axis and the slope of the Williamson–Hall graph, respectively. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c presents the Williamson–Hall plot for the two synthesized material systems with a linear fit line used to determine the intersection value and slope of the curve. The average crystallite size (<i>L</i>) of the B-NLMC material is estimated to be approximately 241 nm, which is 1.6% smaller than 245 nm for the BF-NLMC material. Conversely, the microstrain (ε) of the B-NLMC material is 3.3 × 10<sup>–3</sup>, nearly 27% larger than the ε of the BF-NLMC material (2.4 × 10<sup>–3</sup>). This change in <i>L</i> is consistent with experimental observations as it is related to the decrease in ε due to the displacement/change of elements in the crystal lattice structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> This is consistent with experimental observations, as pointed out in previous paragraphs.</div><div class="NLM_p">The morphology of the synthesized material was determined through scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM), as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a,b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/suppl_file/ao4c06248_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a,b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/suppl_file/ao4c06248_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>, the morphology of both B-NLMC and BF-NLMC is polyhedral with dimensions up to several micrometers. In addition, the layered structure is shown when observing SEM images at high resolution (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/suppl_file/ao4c06248_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1c,f</a>), which is in good agreement with the general properties of P2-type layered structural materials. The difference between the microstructures of the two material systems is not discernible through SEM, which may be attributed to the similar atomic structure of the B-NLMC and BF-NLMC materials and the fact that they were synthesized using the same method. High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM) and selected area electron diffraction (SAED) techniques were employed to investigate the local nanoscopic properties of the sample, providing detailed insights into the structural characteristics at the nanoscale. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c,d illustrates high-magnification bright field images of the two samples with obvious crystallized properties at the atomic scale. The HRTEM images of the samples display three similar sets of lattice fringes with <i>d</i>-spacings of 0.21, 0.41, and 0.56 nm, which can be indexed to the (103), (1/3, 1/3, 1/3), and (002) planes of the <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> symmetry, respectively. Moreover, the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e,f exhibit a high degree of crystallinity in both the B-NLMC and BF-NLMC materials, as evidenced by the presence of well-defined circular spots. These patterns correspond to the hexagonal P2-type phase of the two material systems, as indexed by the lattice fringes observed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c,d. The consistency between the SAED patterns and the XRD patterns (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a,b,d) further strongly supports the formation of nanocrystals in both materials.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/asset/images/medium/ao4c06248_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/asset/images/large/ao4c06248_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. TEM, HR-TEM, and SAED images of the B-NLMC sample (a, c, e) and BF-NLMC sample (b, d, f).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/asset/images/large/ao4c06248_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c06248&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/suppl_file/ao4c06248_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>) reveals the presence of Na, Mn, Co, and O elements in the B-NLMC samples, while the BF-NLMC sample contains Na, Mn, Co, F, and O. Notably, B and Li elements were not detected. This absence is attributed to the low atomic number of these elements, which limits their detectability by EDS. The molar ratios of the detected elements are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/suppl_file/ao4c06248_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>. The results indicate that the detected F content is 3.52%, exceeding the calculated value of approximately 2.74% based on the initial precursor composition. In addition, the EDS elemental mappings of the two developed materials reveal that the distribution of each individual element is well incorporated within the materials (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>). The quantitative composition of each element was determined by ICP-MS analysis. The results revealed that the Na:Li:Mn:Co:B molar ratio in both B-NLMC and BF-NLMC samples is approximately 8.06:0.97:7.96:0.48:0.51, which is in good agreement with the molar ratio of the initially used precursors.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/asset/images/medium/ao4c06248_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/asset/images/large/ao4c06248_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. SEM images and corresponding EDS elemental mapping images of B-NLMC (a, a1, a2, a4, a5) and BF-NLMC (b, b1–b5).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/asset/images/large/ao4c06248_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c06248&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The composition and oxidation states of the atoms in the synthesized materials were further evaluated through X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analysis, with the results shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/suppl_file/ao4c06248_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>. As described in the results, the elements Na, Mn, O, B, and C were observed in the survey spectra of both B-NLMC and BF-NLMC. Meanwhile, elements F and Co were not detected in the XPS high-resolution spectra, which could be due to their low content and the insufficient signal recording time for each survey. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a illustrates the XPS high-resolution spectra of Na 1s, where two peaks were found at about 1071.3 eV corresponding to the sodium state in the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> structure and at about 1072.9 eV corresponding to the sodium state in Na<sub>2</sub>O due to sodium segregation, leading to the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>O on the surface. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> The XPS spectra of Mn 2p (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b) of both B-NLMC and BF-NLMC samples demonstrate four peaks at about 655.66–655.95, 653.52–653.50, 644.27–644.63, and 642.20–642.74 eV, corresponding to Mn<sup>4+</sup> of Mn 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, Mn<sup>3+</sup> of Mn 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, Mn<sup>4+</sup> of Mn 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, and Mn<sup>3+</sup> of Mn 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> The redox state of Mn was calculated from the XPS data, indicating that approximately 37–38% of Mn ions exist in the Mn<sup>3+</sup> oxidation state for both the B-NLMC and BF-NLMC samples. This observation suggests that only about 37–38% of Mn ions in the synthesized materials were oxidized to the Mn<sup>4+</sup> state during the first charge cycle. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/asset/images/medium/ao4c06248_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/asset/images/large/ao4c06248_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. XPS high-resolution spectra of Na 1s (a), Mn 2p (b), B 1s (c), and O 1s (d).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/asset/images/large/ao4c06248_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c06248&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The XPS high-resolution spectra of B 1s (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c) appear with only one peak at about 192.4 eV, which corresponds to the B<sup>3+</sup> oxidation state, the same as B<sup>3+</sup> in Na<sub>2</sub>B<sub>4</sub>O<sub>7</sub> and Li<sub>2</sub>B<sub>4</sub>O<sub>7</sub>. Additionally, no peak at about 194 eV was observed, indicating that no B<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> oxide existed in the synthesized materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> The three peaks observed on the high-resolution spectra of O 1s (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d) were determined to come from the lattice oxygen (529.4–529.6 eV), defective oxygen (531.3–531.6 eV), and surface-adsorbed oxygen (at about 534 eV). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> In summary, the agreement between XRD, SEM, SEM-EDS, HR-TEM, SAED, XPS, and ICP-MS results provides reliable evidence that a sodium-lithium-manganese-cobalt oxide material with B or B and F doping with a P2-type layer structure was successfully synthesized.</div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11">3.2.  Electrochemical Properties</h3><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a,b depicts the GCD profiles of the first 3 cycles of the B-NLM and BF-NLM samples at a current density of 10 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. It can be seen that the charge-specific capacity is smaller than the discharge-specific capacity in the first cycle: the charge/discharge-specific capacities of B-NLM and BF-NLM are 115.3/166.5 and 71.5/141.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The larger discharge-specific capacity compared to the charge-specific capacity in the first cycle indicates that the amount of sodium ions extracted from the material structure in the charge process is less than the amount of sodium inserted into the material structure during the discharge process, which explains why the Coulombic efficiency is abnormally high in the first cycle for both B-NLMC and BF-NLMC samples (as depicted in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/suppl_file/ao4c06248_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5c</a>). This phenomenon can be attributed to (i) the stranding of sodium ions due to their large size during loading and (ii) the available vacant sodium-ion sites of the pristine material allowing sodium ions to be inserted into the material structure during discharge in the first cycle. After the first cycle, the GCD profiles for cycles 2 and 3 of both B-NLMC and BF-NLMC samples almost overlap, revealing their high reversibility. However, this high reversibility is unfortunately maintained only for the first 10 cycles, after which the specific capacity gradually decreases in the subsequent cycles, as depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c. After 100 cycles, B-NLMC and BF-NLMC provide specific capacity values of 120 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to 72.1% of the initial capacity, and 100.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to 66.8% of the initial capacity, respectively.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/asset/images/medium/ao4c06248_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/asset/images/large/ao4c06248_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. GCD profiles of B-NLMC (a) and BF-NLMC (b); cycle performance (c) and rate capacity (d) of B-NLMC and BF-NLMC samples.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/asset/images/large/ao4c06248_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c06248&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The diffusion behavior of Na ions during the charging and discharging processes was investigated by using GITT analysis, as depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a,c presents the GITT profiles for the B-NLMC and BF-NLMC samples, respectively. At a pulse current density of 10 mA g<sup>–1</sup> over a duration of 30 min, followed by a 30 min relaxation period, the GITT profiles for both samples closely agree well with their corresponding GCD profiles at the same current density in terms of curve shape and reversibility (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a,b). The diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> ions (<i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math" id="MJXp-Span-120"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-121"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-122" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">D</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-123" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-124"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-125" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">Na</span><span class="MJXp-mo MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-126" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;">+</span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-3"><math display="inline"><msub><mi>D</mi><mrow><msup><mi>Na</mi><mo>+</mo></msup></mrow></msub></math></script>) was calculated from the GITT profiles using the following equation: <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq3"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-127"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-128"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-129"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-130"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-131"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-132" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">D</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-133" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-134"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-135" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">Na</span><span class="MJXp-mo MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-136" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;">+</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-137"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-138" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-139"><span class="MJXp-mfrac" id="MJXp-Span-140" style="vertical-align: 0.25em;"><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-141"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-142">4</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-143" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">×</span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-144"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-145" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">L</span><span class="MJXp-mn MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-146" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;">2</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-box" style="margin-top: -0.9em;"><span class="MJXp-denom"><span><span class="MJXp-rule" style="height: 1em; border-top: none; border-bottom: 1px solid; margin: 0.1em 0px;"></span></span><span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-147"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-148">π</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-149" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">×</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-150">τ</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-151"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-152" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-153" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em; vertical-align: -0.689em;"><span class="MJXp-right MJXp-scale4" style="font-size: 3.756em; margin-left: -0.17em;">(</span></span><span class="MJXp-mfrac" id="MJXp-Span-154" style="vertical-align: 0.25em;"><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-155"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-156">Δ</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-157"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-158" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">E</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-159" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;">s</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-box" style="margin-top: -0.9em;"><span class="MJXp-denom"><span><span class="MJXp-rule" style="height: 1em; border-top: none; border-bottom: 1px solid; margin: 0.1em 0px;"></span></span><span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-160"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-161">Δ</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-162"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-163" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">E</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-164" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;">t</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-165" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em; vertical-align: -0.689em;"><span class="MJXp-right MJXp-scale4" style="font-size: 3.756em; margin-left: -0.17em;">)</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mn MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-166" style="vertical-align: 1.54em;">2</span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-4"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mi>D</mi><mrow><msup><mi>Na</mi><mo>+</mo></msup></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mfrac><mrow><mn>4</mn><mo>×</mo><msup><mi>L</mi><mn>2</mn></msup></mrow><mrow><mi>π</mi><mo>×</mo><mi>τ</mi></mrow></mfrac><msup><mrow><mo stretchy="true">(</mo><mfrac><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><msub><mi>E</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi></msub></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><msub><mi>E</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">t</mi></msub></mrow></mfrac><mo stretchy="true">)</mo></mrow><mn>2</mn></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"></div></div>where <i>L</i> (cm) represents the electrode thickness, τ (min) denotes the pulse duration, Δ<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> (V) is defined as the steady-state potential change (excluding <i>iR</i> drop), and Δ<i>E</i><sub>t</sub> (V) refers to the voltage change during the constant current pulse (without <i>iR</i> drop). The variation of <i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math" id="MJXp-Span-167"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-168"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-169" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">D</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-170" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-171"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-172" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">Na</span><span class="MJXp-mo MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-173" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;">+</span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-5"><math display="inline"><msub><mi>D</mi><mrow><msup><mi>Na</mi><mo>+</mo></msup></mrow></msub></math></script> during both charging and discharging is illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b,d, as well as in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/suppl_file/ao4c06248_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>. In the first cycle, the <i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math" id="MJXp-Span-174"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-175"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-176" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">D</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-177" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-178"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-179" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">Na</span><span class="MJXp-mo MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-180" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;">+</span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-6"><math display="inline"><msub><mi>D</mi><mrow><msup><mi>Na</mi><mo>+</mo></msup></mrow></msub></math></script> values during both charging and discharging for the BF-NLMC sample significantly surpass those of the B-NLMC sample. However, the differences in material structural stability led to notable variation of <i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math" id="MJXp-Span-181"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-182"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-183" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">D</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-184" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-185"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-186" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">Na</span><span class="MJXp-mo MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-187" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;">+</span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-7"><math display="inline"><msub><mi>D</mi><mrow><msup><mi>Na</mi><mo>+</mo></msup></mrow></msub></math></script> values during the second cycle for both samples. Specifically, while the <i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math" id="MJXp-Span-188"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-189"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-190" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">D</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-191" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-192"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-193" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">Na</span><span class="MJXp-mo MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-194" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;">+</span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-8"><math display="inline"><msub><mi>D</mi><mrow><msup><mi>Na</mi><mo>+</mo></msup></mrow></msub></math></script> value of the B-NLMC sample with B doping shows a minimal change compared to the first cycle, substantial variation is observed for BF-NLMC with B/F codoping. In this second cycle, the <i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math" id="MJXp-Span-195"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-196"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-197" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">D</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-198" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-199"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-200" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">Na</span><span class="MJXp-mo MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-201" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;">+</span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-9"><math display="inline"><msub><mi>D</mi><mrow><msup><mi>Na</mi><mo>+</mo></msup></mrow></msub></math></script> fluctuates erratically during charging, particularly within the voltage range of 2.3–3 V, and tends to decrease relative to its first-cycle values at equivalent voltage levels (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b). Meanwhile, during discharging, <i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math" id="MJXp-Span-202"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-203"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-204" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">D</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-205" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-206"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-207" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">Na</span><span class="MJXp-mo MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-208" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;">+</span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-10"><math display="inline"><msub><mi>D</mi><mrow><msup><mi>Na</mi><mo>+</mo></msup></mrow></msub></math></script> decreases sharply but remains higher than that of the B-NLMC sample (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>d). The superior sodium-ion diffusion coefficient of the BF-NLMC sample is considered a primary factor contributing to its enhanced rate capacity performance. Furthermore, an overall evaluation of <i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math" id="MJXp-Span-209"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-210"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-211" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">D</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-212" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-213"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-214" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">Na</span><span class="MJXp-mo MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-215" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;">+</span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-11"><math display="inline"><msub><mi>D</mi><mrow><msup><mi>Na</mi><mo>+</mo></msup></mrow></msub></math></script> variations in the initial two cycles indicates that the B-NLMC sample with B doping exhibits greater structural stability compared to the BF-NLMC sample with B/F codoping, thereby elucidating why the cycling performance of B-NLMC is superior.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/asset/images/medium/ao4c06248_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/asset/images/large/ao4c06248_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. GITT profiles of the B-NLMC sample (a) and BF-NLMC sample (c); <i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math" id="MJXp-Span-216"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-217"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-218" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">D</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-219" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-220"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-221" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">Na</span><span class="MJXp-mo MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-222" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;">+</span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-12"><math display="inline"><msub><mi>D</mi><mrow><msup><mi>Na</mi><mo>+</mo></msup></mrow></msub></math></script> value in the charge process (b) and discharge process (d).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/asset/images/large/ao4c06248_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c06248&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">CV analysis was conducted to elucidate the mechanisms and kinetics of the electrochemical processes involved. As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a,c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/suppl_file/ao4c06248_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5d</a>, a pronounced redox couple is evident in the CV curves of both B-NLMC and BF-NLMC samples at potentials around 2.4/1.9 V, corresponding to the Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox couple. This finding is coherent with previous studies on similar materials, such as Na<sub>0.67</sub>[Fe<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> Na<sub>2.4</sub>Mn<sub>2.6</sub>Al<sub>0.4</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> and P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.9</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> Compared with some other previously reported similar materials such as P2-Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.167</sub>Co<sub>0.167</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> P2-Na<sub>0.67</sub>[Mn<sub>0.65</sub>Co<sub>0.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.15</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> and P2-Na<sub>0.67</sub>Co<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.75–<i>x</i></sub>Nb<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> a pair of less intense peaks observed above 3.5 V is attributed to the Co<sup>3+</sup>/Co<sup>4+</sup> redox couple. Additionally, other pairs of weakly intense peaks at 2.80/2.66 and 3.18/3.02 V, as depicted in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/suppl_file/ao4c06248_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5d</a>, might indicate phase transitions. However, the precise origins of these peaks remain unclear, necessitating further investigation for resolution.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/asset/images/medium/ao4c06248_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/asset/images/large/ao4c06248_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. CV curves at different scan rates and Randles–Sevcik line of the B-NLMC sample (a, b) and BF-NLMC sample (c, d).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/asset/images/large/ao4c06248_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c06248&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a,c demonstrates a slight peak shift toward higher potentials for the anodic process and toward lower potentials for the cathodic process as the scan rate increases, indicating kinetic limitations of the electrochemical processes. Notably, the current at the redox peaks rises with increasing scan rates and exhibits a linear relationship with the square root of the scan rate, as is evidenced in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b,d. This behavior, described by the Randles–Sevcik equation, suggests that the electrochemical reactions are rapid and highly reversible, primarily governed by the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. The Randles–Sevcik equation is expressed as follows: <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq4"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-223"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-224"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-225"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-226"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-227"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-228" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">I</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-229" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;">p</span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-230"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-231" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-232"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-233">2.69</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-234" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">×</span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-235"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-236" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">10</span><span class="MJXp-mn MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-237" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;">5</span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-238" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">×</span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-239"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-240" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">n</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-241" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-242">3</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-243">/</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-244">2</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-245" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">×</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-246">A</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-247" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">×</span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-248"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-249" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">D</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-250" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-251">1</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-252">/</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-253">2</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-254" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">×</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-255">C</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-256" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">×</span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-257"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-258" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">ν</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-259" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-260">1</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-261">/</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-262">2</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-13"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mi>I</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">p</mi></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mn>2.69</mn><mo>×</mo><msup><mn>10</mn><mn>5</mn></msup><mo>×</mo><msup><mi>n</mi><mrow><mn>3</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><mo>×</mo><mi>A</mi><mo>×</mo><msup><mi>D</mi><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><mo>×</mo><mi>C</mi><mo>×</mo><msup><mi>ν</mi><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"></div></div>where <i>n</i> represents the number of exchanged electrons, <i>A</i> (cm<sup>2</sup>) denotes the electrode area, <i>D</i> (cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>) is the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient, and <i>C</i> is the Na<sup>+</sup> ion concentration. Accordingly, the <i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math" id="MJXp-Span-263"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-264"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-265" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">D</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-266" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-267"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-268" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">Na</span><span class="MJXp-mo MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-269" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;">+</span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-14"><math display="inline"><msub><mi>D</mi><mrow><msup><mi>Na</mi><mo>+</mo></msup></mrow></msub></math></script> values during the anodic and cathodic processes for B-NLMC and BF-NLMC samples are calculated to be 3.79/0.69 × 10<sup>–8</sup> and 3.99/0.72 × 10<sup>–8</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. It is important to note that the first CV curve in this analysis typically does not close. Thus, data for CV analysis are extracted from the second cycle, corresponding to the subsequent charge–discharge process. The results reveal that the <i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math" id="MJXp-Span-270"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-271"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-272" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">D</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-273" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-274"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-275" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">Na</span><span class="MJXp-mo MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-276" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;">+</span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-15"><math display="inline"><msub><mi>D</mi><mrow><msup><mi>Na</mi><mo>+</mo></msup></mrow></msub></math></script> values for both synthesized materials are equivalent, which is consistent with GITT analysis and their structural characteristics, thereby confirming the high reliability of these findings.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/suppl_file/ao4c06248_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a,b</a> shows the GCD profiles of the first cycle and the 100th cycle of synthesized materials. It can be seen that the GCD profiles of the 100th cycle are not as smooth as the first cycle, with noise points appearing like sawtooth shapes, indicating that the process of intercalation/deinterleaving sodium ions is difficult. In addition, the voltage drop phenomenon at the beginning of the discharge process in the 100th cycle increased significantly compared to the first cycle, which is attributed to the increase in resistance of the entire coin-cell CR-2032 system, including material resistance and charge-transfer resistance. The above observations are the reason for the decreasing specific capacities of B-NLMC and BF-NLMC after the cycling efficiency test, which will be discussed in detail in the next section. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d presents the result of evaluating the electrochemical performance of the synthesized materials at different current densities (10, 20, 50, 100, and 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). In contrast to the energy storage capacity and cycling efficiency presented above, the BF-NLMC sample exhibits better rate capacity than the B-NLMC sample. Specifically, when the current density increases from 10 to 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the specific capacity of the BF-NLMC sample decreases from 148.3 to 79.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, while the specific capacity of the B-NLMC sample decreases from 166.4 to 30.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. The above results show that the influence of doping elements on the electrochemical performance is very different. While doping B into sodium-lithium-manganese-cobalt oxide improves the energy storage capacity of the resulting material, the simultaneous doping of B and F reduces the specific capacity compared to the B-NLMC sample but improves the rate capacity. The GITT and CV analyses indicate that the Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion coefficient in the first two cycles of the BF-NLMC sample with B/F codoping is better than the B-NLMC sample with B doping, which is clearly explained why the rate capacity of the B-NLMC sample is better than that of the BF-NLMC sample. Previous studies have highlighted the beneficial role of F<sup>–</sup> anion in enhancing rate capacity performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28 ref32" aria-label="reference 28 and 32">(28,32)</a> Specifically, the F<sup>–</sup> anion can substitute for O<sup>2–</sup>, thereby increasing electron density, reducing material resistance, and lowering the energy barrier for Na<sup>+</sup> ion migration. The electrochemical performance of both B-NLMC and BF-NLMC samples surpasses that of several similar materials, including those without doping or with alternative doping strategies, such as P2-Na<sub>1.0</sub>Li<sub>0.2</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> P2-Na<sub>0.8</sub>Li<sub>0.2</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> P2-Na<sub>45/54</sub>Li<sub>4/54</sub>Ni<sub>16/54</sub>Mn<sub>34/54</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> and P2-Na<sub>0.67</sub>(Mn<sub>0.55</sub>Ni<sub>0.21</sub>Co<sub>0.24</sub>)O<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> However, their cycling performance is comparatively limited when juxtaposed with certain materials such as P2-Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> cobalt-doped sodium-lithium-manganese oxide, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> O3-Na(Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>)<sub>1–<i>x</i></sub>Ti<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> and P2-Na<sub>45/54</sub>Mg<sub>6/54</sub>Ni<sub>12/54</sub>Mn<sub>34/54</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> The above results can be explained as follows: (i) As the XRD results showed, the lattice parameters of B-NLMC and BF-NLMC are larger than those of sodium-manganese oxide-based materials such as P2-Na<sub>0.78</sub>Ni<sub>0.32</sub>Mn<sub>0.68</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> P2/O3-Na<sub>0.66</sub>Li<sub>0.18</sub>Mn<sub>0.71</sub>Ni<sub>0.21</sub>Co<sub>0.08</sub>O<sub>2+δ</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a> and Li<sup>+</sup>/F<sup>–</sup>-codoped Na<sub>2/3</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>(Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>)<sub>0.9</sub>O<sub>1.95</sub>F<sub>0.05</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> leading to easier migration of sodium ions. Consequently, both B-NLMC and BF-NLMC demonstrate high energy storage capabilities at small current densities. (ii) Too large lattice parameters will reduce structural stability during the sodium-ion intercalation/deintercalation process, which leads to a cyclic decrease in specific capacity. Moreover, undesirable reactions such as the reaction between the electrode material and the electrolyte and the reactions of electrolyte decomposition at high voltages are among the main causes of reduced cycle efficiency. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 16 and 17">(16,17)</a> The products of these reactions accumulate on the electrode surface, leading to increased resistance of the electrode and charge transport resistance and hindering the diffusion of sodium ions. It should be noted that the B and F doping ratio in this work has not been optimized, so this may also be the reason why the cycling performance of the obtained material is not as expected. However, the influence of the doping factor on electrochemical performance is obvious. Therefore, more independent studies are required to address this issue. In addition, the electrolyte also has a great influence on the electrochemical performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58 ref59 ref60" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref58 ref59 ref60" aria-label="reference 58 to 60">(58−60)</a> The different electrolytes will exhibit different redox kinetics, reductive stability of the anions, and properties of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI), which determine the rate capacity and cycle performance. In this study, the NaClO<sub>4</sub> solution in a mixture of ethylene carbonate/diethylene carbonate (EC/DEC, volume ratio of 1:1) with a molar concentration of 1 M is used as an electrolyte. NaClO<sub>4</sub> is known to have better conductivity and ionic mobility than some other electrolytes such as NaPF<sub>6</sub>, NaBF<sub>4</sub>, and NaSO<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>3</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58 ref61" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref58 ref61" aria-label="reference 58 and 61">(58,61)</a> which help both B-NLMC and BF-NLMC samples to exhibit better specific capacity and rate capacity than material systems using other electrolytes. However, the poor reduction stability of ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>–</sup> anion leads to the formation of a solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer on the cathode electrode with excessive growth due to the continuous decomposition of ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>–</sup> anion, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref62" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref62" aria-label="reference 62">(62)</a> which may well explain why the cycle performance of both B-NLMC and BF-NLMC samples has not been as expected. With the above analysis, we believe that the optimization of the electrolyte allows significantly improving the cycle performance of the B-NLMC sample with B doping and the BF-NLMC sample with B/F codoping.</div><div class="NLM_p">The structural change of the material after the cycling test was also evaluated by the XRD analysis of the electrode after 100 cycles. The obtained results were compared with the pristine material as presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/suppl_file/ao4c06248_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>. The XRD pattern of both B-NLMC and BF-NLMC materials after cycling shows all the characteristic peaks of P2-structured material with the hexagonal crystal system and space group <i>P</i>63/<i>mmc</i> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>). However, the intensity of the peak at about 65.3° increases dramatically compared to the pristine materials. This is due to the 2θ positions of the (110) plane of synthesized material and the (220) plane of Al sheet (collector) coinciding. In addition, two impurity peaks at 12.7 and 25.5° on the XRD pattern after the cycling test are believed to be related to the products of unwanted reactions at high voltages during charging. The variation of the lattice parameters before and after the cycling test is summarized in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/suppl_file/ao4c06248_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>. While the distance between transition metal oxide layers (parameter <i>c</i>) after the cycling test did not change significantly, the distance between transition metal atoms (parameter <i>a</i>) within a transition metal oxide layer decreased sharply. The overall result is that the unit cell volume is reduced by 2.2% for the B-NLMC sample and 1.5% for the BF-NLMC sample. The decrease in the unit cell volume is also the reason for the reduction in specific capacity, as there is insufficient space to accommodate the sodium ions. In addition, the morphological changes of the material after one and five cycles were investigated, as presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/suppl_file/ao4c06248_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/suppl_file/ao4c06248_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6a,d</a>, the morphology of active materials remains largely unchanged in the pristine electrode state compared to their original material, although the surface appears rougher (as indicated in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/suppl_file/ao4c06248_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). This roughening is attributed to the influence of the binder (PVDF) and the conductive agent (Super P). After one and five cycles, the active materials maintain their original shape and size, as depicted in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/suppl_file/ao4c06248_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6b–f</a>. However, although the separation between particles is less distinct, there is an observable increase in adhesion and bonding among them with successive cycles. The surface variations of the working electrode after different charge and discharge cycles can be attributed to two main factors: (i) the amount of electrolyte solution adhering to the electrode surface postdrying and (ii) the accumulation of byproducts from unwanted reactions occurring in the high-voltage region. Overall, both B-NLMC and BF-NLMC samples exhibit structural stability on their surfaces during the initial five cycles, consistent with the minimal specific capacity decrease observed during these early cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c). However, this observation alone does not suffice to draw definitive conclusions regarding morphological stability throughout the entire cycling process. Therefore, further independent studies are warranted to address this matter comprehensively.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/asset/images/medium/ao4c06248_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/asset/images/large/ao4c06248_0008.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. XRD results of B-NLMC (a) and BF-NLMC (b) electrodes before and after the cycling test.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/asset/images/large/ao4c06248_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c06248&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) analysis was also performed on a coin cell (CR2032, half-cell) to offer insights into the kinetic and electrochemical behavior of electrode materials, with the results displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/suppl_file/ao4c06248_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>. As depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a>a, the Nyquist spectra of B-NLMC and BF-NLMC electrodes are similar, consisting of (i) a semicircle in the high-to-midfrequency range corresponding to the material resistance (<i>R</i><sub>s</sub>), the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>), and the electrical double layer (EDL) and (ii) a straight line in the low-frequency region corresponding to the Warburg impedance (<i>W</i>). After 35 cycles of rate capacity performance testing, the Nyquist plots of the synthesized material remained in the same shape compared to the pristine electrode before charge–discharge, but the semicircle was widened (as depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a>c), which revealed that the material resistance and charge transfer resistance increased after the cycling test.</div><figure id="fig9" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 9</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/asset/images/medium/ao4c06248_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/asset/images/large/ao4c06248_0009.jpeg" id="gr9" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 9. EIS analysis results: Nyquist plots of synthesized materials before (a) and after (c) cycle testing; <i>Z</i>′–<i>w</i><sup>1/2</sup> plots of synthesized materials before (b) and after (d) cycle testing.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/asset/images/large/ao4c06248_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c06248&amp;id=fig9"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The circuit diagram and values of components are determined by fitting methods through ZPlot software, with the results shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/suppl_file/ao4c06248_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7 and Table S3</a>. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/suppl_file/ao4c06248_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Tables S3</a> shows that the <i>R</i><sub>s</sub>/<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values of BF-NLMC (3.93/24.4 Ω) are slightly smaller than those of B-NLMC (4.26/32.55 Ω), which is in good agreement with the size of the semicircle observed in the Nyquist plots (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a>a). However, this difference is not large enough to confirm that it results from a difference in doping elements but may stem from a small difference in the amount of active material loading on the electrode or an error in the process of fitting the circuit diagram. The parameters related to the material resistance and charge transfer resistance of both B-NLMC and BF-NLMC samples increased significantly after 35 cycles. Specifically, the <i>R</i><sub>s</sub> value increased about 1.6–2.5 times, while <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> increased about 53–60 times. The literature has shown that the unwanted reactions often occur during charging at high voltages and are irreversible, so the products of these reactions accumulate on the electrode surface, which increases material resistance and hinders charge transfer on the electrode surface. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 16 and 17">(16,17)</a> This was mentioned in the discussion of cycling efficiency. The increasing <i>R</i><sub>s</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values lead to a rapid voltage drop at the beginning of the discharge process as depicted in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/suppl_file/ao4c06248_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a, b</a> and the kinetics of the electrochemical reaction are slower than in the first cycles. As a result, the specific capacity gradually decreases.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The impedance Warburg coefficient (σ) is determined by constructing a linear relationship between <i>Z</i>′ and the square root of the reciprocal (ω<sup>–0.5</sup>) at lower angular frequency. As depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a>b,d, the linear relationship is established as exactly described by the equation <i>Z</i>′ = <i>R</i> + σ × ω<sup>–0.5</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref63 ref64" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref63 ref64" aria-label="reference 63 and 64">(63,64)</a> The σ values of B-NLMC and BF-NLMC are defined as 184.45 and 164.07, respectively; thus, it is possible to compare the sodium-ion diffusion coefficient of the synthesized materials according to the equation <i>D</i> = (<i>R</i><sup>2</sup> × <i>T</i><sup>2</sup>)/(<i>S</i><sup>2</sup> × <i>n</i><sup>4</sup> × <i>F</i><sup>4</sup> × <i>C</i><sup>2</sup> × σ<sup>2</sup>), where <i>R</i>, <i>T</i>, <i>F</i>, <i>S</i>, <i>n</i>, and <i>C</i> represent the universal gas constant (8.314 J mol<sup>–1</sup> K<sup>–1</sup>), absolute room temperature (298 K), Faraday constant (96,500 C mol<sup>–1</sup>), electrode surface area (0.785 cm<sup>2</sup>), number of electrons transferred in a redox reaction, and molar concentration of Na<sup>+</sup> ion in B-NLMC or BF-NLMC, respectively. Accordingly, the sodium-ion diffusion coefficient of the BF-NLMC sample is smaller than that of B-NLMC, which explains why the rate capacity performance of the BF-NLMC material is better, while its specific capacity is lower. After 35 cycles, the σ values increase from 184.45 to 388.33 for the B-NLMC sample and from 164.07 to 398.34 for BF-NLMC, which shows that the diffusion coefficient decreased significantly after the rate capacity test. As mentioned above, the products of unwanted reactions accumulated on the electrode surface not only increase material resistance and hinder charge transfer but also obstruct the diffusion of sodium ions into the bulk structure of the material, which is considered the main reason for the decrease in the diffusion coefficient. It is worth noting that although the <i>D</i> value of the BF-NLMC sample is larger than that of B-NLMC when evaluated on the coin cell before charge/discharge, it is smaller when evaluated on the coin cell after 35 cycles. The greater increase in <i>D</i> value of BF-NLMC compared to B-NLMC is evidence that clearly explains why the cycling efficiency of BF-NLMC is lower, even though its rate capacity is better.</div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i25">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, the B or B/F dopants have been successfully incorporated into the crystal structure of sodium-lithium-manganese-cobalt oxide materials. The structural, morphological, and electrochemical properties of the synthesized materials were thoroughly investigated. The obtained results reveal that both the boron-doped (B-NLMC) and boron-fluorine-codoped (BF-NLMC) sodium-lithium-manganese-cobalt oxide materials exhibit a well-defined P2-type layered structure. These materials demonstrate superior electrochemical performance, with maximum specific capacities ranging from 149 to 166 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 10 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Impressively, they maintain approximately 66.8–72.1% of their initial capacity after 100 charge–discharge cycles. In addition, the simultaneous doping of B and F in the BF-NLMC material enhances the sodium-ion diffusion coefficient and rate capability performance. However, a rapid increase in material resistance and charge transfer resistance during cycling leads to lower cycling efficiency compared with the B-NLMC material. This research provides valuable insights into the strategic use of dopants to enhance the structural stability and electrochemical performance of cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries. By optimizing the doping concentrations and exploring alternative dopant combinations, researchers can further improve the energy density, rate capability, and cycling stability of these materials. This paves the way for the development of high-performance sodium-ion batteries for various energy storage applications.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes2"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i26">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Morphologies of the synthesized materials observed by SEM with low and high magnifications; EDS spectrum and characterized elemental composition ratios of the developed samples; cross-sectional SEM images depicting the thicknesses of the active material layers on the electrodes; XPS survey spectrum; GCD profiles after 100 cycles, CV curves, and Coulombic efficiencies of the synthesized materials; morphologies of the pristine electrode and electrode after first and fifth cycles; equivalent electrical circuit diagram of the B-NLMC and BF-NLMC electrodes before and after rate capacity testing; summaries showing values of lattice parameters, diffusion coefficient (<i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math" id="MJXp-Span-277"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-278"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-279" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">D</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-280" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-281"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-282" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">Na</span><span class="MJXp-mo MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-283" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;">+</span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-16"><math display="inline"><msub><mi>D</mi><mrow><msup><mi>Na</mi><mo>+</mo></msup></mrow></msub></math></script>), and parameters in the equivalent electrical circuit diagrams of the synthesized materials (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/suppl_file/ao4c06248_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c06248/suppl_file/ao4c06248_si_001.pdf">ao4c06248_si_001.pdf (1.2 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsomega.4c06248" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1016/j.ensm.2022.03.039"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.</span></span><span class="NLM_etal"></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Understanding rechargeable magnesium ion batteries via first-principles computations: A comprehensive review</span>. <i>Energy Storage Materials</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>48</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">344</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">355</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

Chem., Int. Ed.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>59</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">34</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">14511</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">14516</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Realizing Complete Solid-Solution Reaction in High Sodium Content P2-Type Cathode for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jin, Ting; Wang, Peng-Fei; Wang, Qin-Chao; Zhu, Kunjie; Deng, Tao; Zhang, Jiaxun; Zhang, Wei; Yang, Xiao-Qing; Jiao, Lifang; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">59</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">34</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">14511-14516</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACIEF5</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1433-7851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">P2-type layered oxides suffer from an ordered Na+/vacancy arrangement and P2→O2/OP4 phase transitions, leading them to exhibit multiple voltage plateaus upon Na+ extn./insertion.  The deficient sodium in the P2-type cathode easily induces the bad structural stability at deep desodiation states and limited reversible capacity during Na+ de/insertion.  These drawbacks cause poor rate capability and fast capacity decay in most P2-type layered oxides.  To address these challenges, a novel high sodium content (0.85) and plateau-free P2-type cathode-Na0.85Li0.12Ni0.22Mn0.66O2 (P2-NLNMO) was developed.  The complete solid-soln. reaction over a wide voltage range ensures both fast Na+ mobility (10-11 to 10-10 cm2 s-1) and small vol. variation (1.7%).  The high sodium content P2-NLNMO exhibits a higher reversible capacity of 123.4 mA h g-1, superior rate capability of 79.3 mA h g-1 at 20 C, and 85.4% capacity retention after 500 cycles at 5 C.  The sufficient Na and complete solid-soln. reaction are crit. to realizing high-performance P2-type cathodes for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1016/0378-4363(80)90214-4"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Delmas, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fouassier, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hagenmuller, P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Structural classification and properties of the layered oxides</span>. <i>Physica B+C</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1980</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>99</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">1</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">81</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">85</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/0378-4363(80)90214-4</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D99%26publication_year%3D1980%26pages%3D81-85%26journal%3DPhysica%2BB%252BC%26issue%3D1%26author%3DC.%2BDelmas%26author%3DC.%2BFouassier%26author%3DP.%2BHagenmuller%26title%3DStructural%2Bclassification%2Band%2Bproperties%2Bof%2Bthe%2Blayered%2Boxides%26doi%3D10.1016%252F0378-4363%252880%252990214-4&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsomega.4c06248&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2F0378-4363%2880%2990214-4&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 (space group: R3‾m), having an O3-type (α-NaFeO2 type) structure according to the Delmas' notation, is prepd. by a solid-state method.  The electrochem. reactivity of NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 is examd. in an aprotic sodium cell at room temp.  The NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 electrodes can deliver ca. 105-125 mAh g-1 at rates of 240-4.8 mA g-1 in the voltage range of 2.2-3.8 V and show 75% of the initial reversible capacity after 50 charge/discharge cycling tests.  In the voltage range of 2.2-4.5 V, a higher reversible capacity of 185 mAh g-1 is achieved; however, its reversibility is insufficient because of the significant expansion of interslab space by charging to 4.5 V vs. sodium.  The reversibility is improved by adding fluoroethylene carbonate into the electrolyte soln.  The structural transition mechanism of Na1-xNi0.5Mn0.5O2 is also examd. by an ex situ X-ray diffraction method combined with X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS).  The staking sequence of the [Ni0.5Mn0.5]O2 slabs changes progressively as sodium ions are extd. from the crystal lattice.  It is obsd. that the original O3 phase transforms into the O'3, P3, P'3, and P3'' phases during sodium extn.  XAS measurement proves that NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 consists of divalent nickel and tetravalent manganese ions.  As sodium ions are extd. from the oxide to form Na1-xNi0.5Mn0.5O2, nickel ions are oxidized to the trivalent state, while the manganese ions are electrochem. inactive as the tetravalent state.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref11" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1016/j.cap.2018.08.011"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, R.</span></span><span class="NLM_etal"></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">First-principles investigation of the monoclinic NaMnO2 cathode material for rechargeable Na-ion batteries</span>. <i>Curr.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered transition metal compds. have attracted much attention due to their high theor. capacity and energy d. for sodium ion batteries.  However, this kind of material suffers from serious irreversible capacity decay during the charge and discharge process.  Here, using synchrotron-based operando transmission X-ray microscopy and high-energy X-ray diffraction combined with electrochem. measurements, the visualization of the dissym. phase transformation and structure evolution mechanism of layered NaNiO2 material during initial charge and discharge cycles are clarified.  Phase transformation and deformation of NaNiO2 during the voltage range of below 3.0 V and over 4.0 V are responsible for the irreversible capacity loss during the first cycling, which is also confirmed by the evolution of reaction kinetics behavior obtained by the galvanostatic intermittent titrn. technique. A combination of 1st principles computation, electrochem. and synchrotron characterizations is conducted to elucidate the working mechanism for the improved electrochem. properties.  The reversible phase transformation from P2 to O2 is obsd.  New configurations of Na-ions and vacancy are found at x = 1/3 and 1/2, which correspond to the intermediate phases upon the electrochem. cycling process.  The mobility of Na-ions is studied using the galvanostatic intermittent titrn. technique (GITT) and the Na diffusion barriers are calcd. by the Nudged Elastic Band (NEB) method.
